<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Anesthesia</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">State of medically-controlled temporary loss of sensation or awareness</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Paresthesia" title="Paresthesia">Paresthesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anesthetic" title="Anesthetic">Anesthetic</a>. For the medical speciality, see <a href="/wiki/Anesthesiology" title="Anesthesiology">Anesthesiology</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Anesthesia_(disambiguation)" title="Anesthesia (disambiguation)">Anesthesia (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Anesthesia</b> is a state of controlled, temporary loss of sensation or awareness that is induced for medical and veterinary purposes. It may include some or all of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analgesia" title="Analgesia">analgesia</a> (relief from or prevention of <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a>), <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a> (muscle relaxation), <a href="/wiki/Amnesia" title="Amnesia">amnesia</a> (loss of memory), and <a href="/wiki/Unconsciousness" title="Unconsciousness">unconsciousness</a>. An animal under the effects of <a href="/wiki/Anesthetic" title="Anesthetic">anesthetic</a> drugs is referred to as being <b>anesthetized</b>.
</p><p>Anesthesia enables the painless performance of procedures that would otherwise cause severe or intolerable pain to an animal that is not anesthetized, or would otherwise be technically unfeasible. Three broad categories of anesthesia exist:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/General_anaesthesia" title="General anaesthesia">General anesthesia</a> suppresses <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> activity and results in unconsciousness and total lack of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensation_(psychology)" title="Sensation (psychology)">sensation</a>, using either injected or inhaled drugs.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sedation" title="Sedation">Sedation</a> suppresses the central nervous system to a lesser degree, inhibiting both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anxiolysis" title="Anxiolysis">anxiety</a> and creation of <a href="/wiki/Long-term_memory" title="Long-term memory">long-term memories</a> without resulting in unconsciousness.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Local_anesthesia" title="Local anesthesia">Regional and local anesthesia</a>, which blocks transmission of nerve impulses from a specific part of the body. Depending on the situation, this may be used either on its own (in which case the animal remains fully conscious), or in combination with general anesthesia or sedation. Drugs can be targeted at <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nerves</a> to anesthetize an isolated part of the body only, such as numbing a tooth for dental work or using a <a href="/wiki/Nerve_block" title="Nerve block">nerve block</a> to inhibit sensation in an entire limb. Alternatively, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidural_anaesthesia" title="Epidural anaesthesia">epidural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spinal_anaesthesia" title="Spinal anaesthesia">spinal anesthesia</a> can be performed in the region of the central nervous system itself, suppressing all incoming sensation from nerves supplying the area of the block.</li></ul>
<p>In preparing for a medical or veterinary procedure, the clinician chooses one or more drugs to achieve the types and degree of anesthesia characteristics appropriate for the type of procedure and the particular individual. The types of drugs used include <a href="/wiki/General_anaesthetic" title="General anaesthetic">general anesthetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Local_anesthetic" title="Local anesthetic">local anesthetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypnotic" title="Hypnotic">hypnotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dissociative" title="Dissociative">dissociatives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sedative" title="Sedative">sedatives</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adjunct_therapy" title="Adjunct therapy">adjuncts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuromuscular-blocking_drug" title="Neuromuscular-blocking drug">neuromuscular-blocking drugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narcotic" title="Narcotic">narcotics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">analgesics</a>.
</p><p>The risks of complications during or after anesthesia are often difficult to separate from those of the procedure for which anesthesia is being given, but in the main they are related to three factors: the health of the animal, the complexity (and stress) of the procedure itself, and the anaesthetic technique. Of these factors, the health of the animal has the greatest impact. Major perioperative risks can include death, <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attack</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_embolism" title="Pulmonary embolism">pulmonary embolism</a> whereas minor risks can include <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">postoperative nausea and vomiting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hospital_readmission" title="Hospital readmission">hospital readmission</a>. Some conditions, like <a href="/wiki/Local_anesthetic" title="Local anesthetic">local anesthetic</a> toxicity, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airway" title="Airway">airway</a> trauma or <a href="/wiki/Malignant_hyperthermia" title="Malignant hyperthermia">malignant hyperthermia</a>, can be more directly attributed to specific anesthetic drugs and techniques.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_uses">Medical uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Medical uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The purpose of anesthesia can be distilled down to three basic goals or endpoints:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 236">: 236 </span></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hypnotic" title="Hypnotic">hypnosis</a> (a temporary loss of <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a> and with it a loss of <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a>.  In a pharmacological context, the word hypnosis usually has this technical meaning, in contrast to its more familiar lay or psychological meaning of an altered state of consciousness not necessarily caused by drugs—see <a href="/wiki/Hypnosis" title="Hypnosis">hypnosis</a>).</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analgesia" title="Analgesia">analgesia</a> (lack of sensation which also blunts <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">autonomic reflexes</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Muscle_relaxant" title="Muscle relaxant">muscle relaxation</a></li></ul>
<p>Different types of anesthesia affect the endpoints differently. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regional_anesthesia" title="Regional anesthesia">Regional anesthesia</a>, for instance, affects analgesia; <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepine</a>-type sedatives (used for sedation, or "<a href="/wiki/Twilight_anesthesia" title="Twilight anesthesia">twilight anesthesia</a>") favor <a href="/wiki/Amnesia" title="Amnesia">amnesia</a>; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_anesthesia" title="General anesthesia">general anesthetics</a> can affect all of the endpoints.  The goal of anesthesia is to achieve the endpoints required for the given surgical procedure with the least risk to the animal.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Operating_room_anesthetic_station.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5400" data-file-width="6807" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Operating_room_anesthetic_station.jpg/220px-Operating_room_anesthetic_station.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Operating_room_anesthetic_station.jpg/330px-Operating_room_anesthetic_station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Operating_room_anesthetic_station.jpg/440px-Operating_room_anesthetic_station.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Operating_room_anesthetic_station.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The anesthetic area of an operating room</div></div></div>
<p>To achieve the goals of anesthesia, drugs act on different but interconnected parts of the nervous system. Hypnosis, for instance, is generated through actions on the <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_(neuroanatomy)" title="Nucleus (neuroanatomy)">nuclei in the brain</a> and is similar to the activation of <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a>.  The effect is to make people less <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">aware</a> and less reactive to <a href="/wiki/Noxious_stimulus" title="Noxious stimulus">noxious stimuli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 245">: 245 </span></sup>
</p><p>Loss of <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> (<a href="/wiki/Amnesia" title="Amnesia">amnesia</a>) is created by action of drugs on multiple (but specific) regions of the brain.  Memories are created as either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Declarative_memory" title="Declarative memory">declarative</a> or <a href="/wiki/Procedural_memory" title="Procedural memory">non-declarative</a> memories in several stages (<a href="/wiki/Short-term_memory" title="Short-term memory">short-term</a>, <a href="/wiki/Long-term_memory" title="Long-term memory">long-term</a>, <a href="/wiki/Working_memory" title="Working memory">long-lasting</a>) the strength of which is determined by the strength of connections between neurons termed <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_plasticity" title="Synaptic plasticity">synaptic plasticity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 246">: 246 </span></sup> Each anesthetic produces amnesia through unique effects on memory formation at variable doses. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inhalational_anesthetics" title="Inhalational anesthetics">Inhalational anesthetics</a> will reliably produce amnesia through general suppression of the nuclei at doses below those required for loss of consciousness. Drugs like <a href="/wiki/Midazolam" title="Midazolam">midazolam</a> produce amnesia through different pathways by blocking the formation of long-term memories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 249">: 249 </span></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, a person can have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dreams" title="Dreams">dreams</a> during anesthetic or have consciousness of the procedure despite having no indication of it under anesthetic.  It is estimated that 22% of people <a href="/wiki/Dream" title="Dream">dream</a> during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_anesthesia" title="General anesthesia">general anesthesia</a> and 1 - 2 cases per 1000 have some consciousness termed "<a href="/wiki/Anesthesia_awareness" title="Anesthesia awareness">awareness during general anesthesia</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 253">: 253 </span></sup> It is unknown whether non-human animals have dreams during general anesthesia.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Techniques">Techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Anesthesia is unique in that it is not a direct means of treatment; rather, it allows the <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">doctor</a> or <a href="/wiki/Veterinarian" title="Veterinarian">veterinarian</a> to do things that may treat, diagnose, or cure an ailment which would otherwise be painful or complicated. The best anesthetic, therefore, is the one with the lowest risk to the patient that still achieves the endpoints required to complete the procedure. The first stage in anesthesia is the pre-operative risk assessment consisting of the <a href="/wiki/Medical_history" title="Medical history">medical history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_examination" title="Physical examination">physical examination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medical_test" title="Medical test">lab tests</a>. Diagnosing a animal's pre-operative physical status allows the clinician to minimize anesthetic risks.  A well completed medical history will arrive at the correct diagnosis 56% of the time which increases to 73% with a physical examination.  <a href="/wiki/Medical_test" title="Medical test">Lab tests</a> help in diagnosis but only in 3% of cases, underscoring the need for a full history and physical examination prior to anesthetics. Incorrect pre-operative assessments or preparations are the root cause of 11% of all adverse anesthetic events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1003">: 1003 </span></sup>
</p><p>Safe anesthesia care depends greatly on well-functioning teams of highly trained healthcare workers. The <a href="/wiki/Medical_specialty" title="Medical specialty">medical specialty</a> centred around anesthesia is called <a href="/wiki/Anesthesiology" title="Anesthesiology">anesthesiology</a>, and doctors specialised in the field are termed anesthesiologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29734240_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29734240-3">[3]</a></sup> Additional healthcare professionals involved in anesthesia provision have varying titles and roles depending on the jurisdiction, and include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perioperative_nurse" title="Perioperative nurse">anesthetic nurses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nurse_anesthetist" title="Nurse anesthetist">nurse anesthetists</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anesthesiologist_assistant" title="Anesthesiologist assistant">anesthesiologist assistants</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaesthetic_technician" title="Anaesthetic technician">anaesthetic technicians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anaesthesia_associate" title="Anaesthesia associate">anaesthesia associates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operating_department_practitioner" title="Operating department practitioner">operating department practitioners</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anesthesia_technologist" title="Anesthesia technologist">anesthesia technologists</a>. International standards for the safe practice of anesthesia, jointly endorsed by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> and the <a href="/wiki/World_Federation_of_Societies_of_Anaesthesiologists" title="World Federation of Societies of Anaesthesiologists">World Federation of Societies of Anaesthesiologists</a>, highly recommend that anesthesia should be provided, overseen or led by anesthesiologists, with the exception of minimal sedation or superficial procedures performed under local anesthesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29734240_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29734240-3">[3]</a></sup> A trained, vigilant anesthesia provider should continually care for the animal; where the <a href="/wiki/Certified_Registered_Nurse_Anesthetist" title="Certified Registered Nurse Anesthetist">provider</a> is not an anesthesiologist, they should be locally directed and supervised by an anesthesiologist, and in countries or settings where this is not feasible, care should be led by the most qualified local individual within a regional or national anesthesiologist-led framework.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29734240_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29734240-3">[3]</a></sup> The same minimum standards for <a href="/wiki/Patient_safety" title="Patient safety">patient safety</a> apply regardless of the provider, including continuous clinical and biometric monitoring of tissue oxygenation, perfusion and blood pressure; confirmation of correct placement of <a href="/wiki/Airway_management" title="Airway management">airway management</a> devices by <a href="/wiki/Auscultation" title="Auscultation">auscultation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> detection; use of the <a href="/wiki/WHO_Surgical_Safety_Checklist" title="WHO Surgical Safety Checklist">WHO Surgical Safety Checklist</a>; and safe onward transfer of the patient's care following the procedure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29734240_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29734240-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable floatright" style="text-align:center;font-size:90%;width:45%;margin-left:1em">

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>One part of the <a href="/wiki/Risk_management" title="Risk management">risk assessment</a> is based on the patients' health.  The American Society of Anesthesiologists has developed a six-tier scale that stratifies the patient's pre-operative physical state.  It is called the <a href="/wiki/ASA_physical_status_classification_system" title="ASA physical status classification system">ASA physical status</a>. The scale assesses risk as the patient's general health relates to an anesthetic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry2011_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Henry2011-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The more detailed pre-operative <a href="/wiki/Medical_history" title="Medical history">medical history</a> aims to discover genetic disorders (such as <a href="/wiki/Malignant_hyperthermia" title="Malignant hyperthermia">malignant hyperthermia</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pseudocholinesterase_deficiency" title="Pseudocholinesterase deficiency">pseudocholinesterase deficiency</a>), habits (<a href="/wiki/Smoking" title="Smoking">tobacco</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_abuse" title="Drug abuse">drug</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dependence" title="Alcohol dependence">alcohol use</a>), physical attributes (such as <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> or a difficult <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airway" title="Airway">airway</a>) and any coexisting diseases (especially <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">respiratory diseases</a>) that might impact the anesthetic.  The <a href="/wiki/Physical_examination" title="Physical examination">physical examination</a> helps quantify the impact of anything found in the medical history in addition to lab tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1003–09">: 1003–09 </span></sup>
</p><p>Aside from the generalities of the patient's health assessment, an evaluation of specific factors as they relate to the surgery also need to be considered for anesthesia.  For instance, anesthesia during <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">childbirth</a> must consider not only the mother but the baby.  <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">Cancers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">tumors</a> that occupy the lungs or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airway" title="Airway">throat</a> create special challenges to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_anesthesia" title="General anesthesia">general anesthesia</a>.  After determining the health of the animal undergoing anesthetic and the endpoints that are required to complete the procedure, the type of anesthetic can be selected. Choice of surgical method and anesthetic technique aims to reduce risk of complications, shorten time needed for recovery and minimize the <a href="/wiki/Surgical_stress" title="Surgical stress">surgical stress</a> response.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_anesthesia">General anesthesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: General anesthesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/General_anaesthesia" title="General anaesthesia">General anaesthesia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_anesthetic" title="General anesthetic">General anesthetic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Inhalational_anesthetic" title="Inhalational anesthetic">Inhalational anesthetic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vaporizer_Sevoflurane_001_JPN.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2309" data-file-width="1635" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Vaporizer_Sevoflurane_001_JPN.jpg/220px-Vaporizer_Sevoflurane_001_JPN.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Vaporizer_Sevoflurane_001_JPN.jpg/330px-Vaporizer_Sevoflurane_001_JPN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Vaporizer_Sevoflurane_001_JPN.jpg/440px-Vaporizer_Sevoflurane_001_JPN.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vaporizer_Sevoflurane_001_JPN.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Vaporizer_(inhalation_device)" title="Vaporizer (inhalation device)">vaporizer</a> holds a liquid anesthetic and converts it to gas for inhalation (in this case <a href="/wiki/Sevoflurane" title="Sevoflurane">sevoflurane</a>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mask_Ventilation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="901" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Mask_Ventilation.jpg/220px-Mask_Ventilation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Mask_Ventilation.jpg/330px-Mask_Ventilation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Mask_Ventilation.jpg/440px-Mask_Ventilation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mask_Ventilation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A patient receiving anesthesia through inhalation</div></div></div>
<p>Anesthesia is a combination of the endpoints (discussed above) that are reached by drugs acting on different but overlapping sites in the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>.  General anesthesia (as opposed to sedation or regional anesthesia) has three main goals: lack of movement (<a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a>), <a href="/wiki/Unconsciousness" title="Unconsciousness">unconsciousness</a>, and blunting of the <a href="/wiki/Fight-or-flight_response" title="Fight-or-flight response">stress response</a>.  In the early days of anesthesia, anesthetics could reliably achieve the first two, allowing surgeons to perform necessary procedures, but many patients died because the extremes of blood pressure and pulse caused by the surgical insult were ultimately harmful.  Eventually, the need for blunting of the <a href="/wiki/Surgical_stress" title="Surgical stress">surgical stress</a> response was identified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvey_Williams_Cushing" title="Harvey Williams Cushing">Harvey Cushing</a>, who injected local anesthetic prior to <a href="/wiki/Hernia_repair" title="Hernia repair">hernia repairs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 30">: 30 </span></sup> This led to the development of other drugs that could blunt the response leading to lower surgical <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rates</a>.
</p><p>The most common approach to reach the endpoints of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_anesthesia" title="General anesthesia">general anesthesia</a> is through the use of inhaled general anesthetics.  Each anesthetic has its own potency which is correlated to its solubility in oil. This relationship exists because the drugs bind directly to cavities in proteins of the central nervous system, although several <a href="/wiki/Theories_of_general_anaesthetic_action" title="Theories of general anaesthetic action">theories of general anesthetic action</a> have been described. Inhalational anesthetics are thought to exact their effects on different parts of the central nervous system.  For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">immobilizing</a> effect of inhaled anesthetics results from an effect on the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a> whereas sedation, hypnosis and amnesia involve sites in the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 515">: 515 </span></sup>  The potency of an inhalational anesthetic is quantified by its <a href="/wiki/Minimum_alveolar_concentration" title="Minimum alveolar concentration">minimum alveolar concentration</a> or MAC.  The MAC is the percentage dose of anesthetic that will prevent a response to painful stimulus in 50% of subjects.  The higher the MAC, generally, the less potent the anesthetic.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anesthesia_medications.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1229" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Anesthesia_medications.JPG/220px-Anesthesia_medications.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Anesthesia_medications.JPG/330px-Anesthesia_medications.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Anesthesia_medications.JPG/440px-Anesthesia_medications.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anesthesia_medications.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Syringe" title="Syringe">Syringes</a> prepared with medications that are expected to be used during an operation under general anesthesia maintained by <a href="/wiki/Sevoflurane" title="Sevoflurane">sevoflurane</a> gas: <br/>– <a href="/wiki/Propofol" title="Propofol">Propofol</a>, a hypnotic <br/>– <a href="/wiki/Ephedrine" title="Ephedrine">Ephedrine</a>, in case of <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">hypotension</a> <br/>– <a href="/wiki/Fentanyl" title="Fentanyl">Fentanyl</a>, for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analgesia" title="Analgesia">analgesia</a> <br/>– <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atracurium" title="Atracurium">Atracurium</a>, for <a href="/wiki/Neuromuscular-blocking_drug" title="Neuromuscular-blocking drug">neuromuscular blockade</a> <br/>– <a href="/wiki/Glycopyrronium_bromide" title="Glycopyrronium bromide">Glycopyrronium bromide</a> (here under trade name "Robinul"), reducing secretions</div></div></div>
<p>The ideal anesthetic drug would provide hypnosis, amnesia, analgesia, and muscle relaxation without undesirable changes in blood pressure, pulse or breathing.  In the 1930s, physicians started to augment inhaled general anesthetics with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injectable" title="Injectable">intravenous</a> general anesthetics.  The drugs used in combination offered a better risk profile to the animal under anesthesia and a quicker recovery.  A combination of drugs was later shown to result in lower odds of dying in the first 7 days after anesthetic. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Propofol" title="Propofol">propofol</a> (injection) might be used to start the anesthetic, <a href="/wiki/Fentanyl" title="Fentanyl">fentanyl</a> (injection) used to blunt the stress response, <a href="/wiki/Midazolam" title="Midazolam">midazolam</a> (injection) given to ensure amnesia and <a href="/wiki/Sevoflurane" title="Sevoflurane">sevoflurane</a> (inhaled) during the procedure to maintain the effects.  More recently, several intravenous drugs have been developed which, if desired, allow inhaled general anesthetics to be avoided completely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 720">: 720 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment">Equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Instruments_used_in_anesthesiology" title="Instruments used in anesthesiology">Instruments used in anesthesiology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anaesthetic_machine" title="Anaesthetic machine">Anaesthetic machine</a></div>
<p>The core instrument in an inhalational anesthetic delivery system is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anesthetic_machine" title="Anesthetic machine">anesthetic machine</a>. It has <a href="/wiki/Anesthetic_vaporizer" title="Anesthetic vaporizer">vaporizers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_ventilator" title="Medical ventilator">ventilators</a>, an anesthetic breathing circuit, waste gas scavenging system and pressure gauges. The purpose of the anesthetic machine is to provide anesthetic gas at a constant pressure, oxygen for breathing and to remove carbon dioxide or other waste anesthetic gases. Since inhalational anesthetics are flammable, various checklists have been developed to confirm that the machine is ready for use, that the safety features are active and the electrical hazards are removed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Machine_checklist_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Machine_checklist-5">[5]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intravenous" title="Intravenous">Intravenous</a> anesthetic is delivered either by <a href="/wiki/Bolus_(medicine)" title="Bolus (medicine)">bolus</a> doses or an <a href="/wiki/Infusion_pump" title="Infusion pump">infusion pump</a>. There are also many smaller instruments used in <a href="/wiki/Airway_management" title="Airway management">airway management</a> and monitoring the patient. The common thread to <a href="/wiki/Certified_Registered_Nurse_Anesthetist" title="Certified Registered Nurse Anesthetist">modern machinery</a> in this field is the use of <a href="/wiki/Fail-safe" title="Fail-safe">fail-safe</a> systems that decrease the odds of catastrophic misuse of the machine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Machine_safety_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Machine_safety-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Monitoring">Monitoring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Monitoring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maquet_Flow-I_anesthesia_machine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3350" data-file-width="2664" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Maquet_Flow-I_anesthesia_machine.jpg/220px-Maquet_Flow-I_anesthesia_machine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Maquet_Flow-I_anesthesia_machine.jpg/330px-Maquet_Flow-I_anesthesia_machine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Maquet_Flow-I_anesthesia_machine.jpg/440px-Maquet_Flow-I_anesthesia_machine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maquet_Flow-I_anesthesia_machine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anesthetic_machine" title="Anesthetic machine">anesthetic machine</a> with integrated systems for <a href="/wiki/Monitoring_(medicine)" title="Monitoring (medicine)">monitoring</a> of several vital parameters.</div></div></div>
<p>Patients under general anesthesia must undergo continuous physiological <a href="/wiki/Monitoring_(medicine)" title="Monitoring (medicine)">monitoring</a> to ensure safety. In the US, the <a href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Anesthesiologists" title="American Society of Anesthesiologists">American Society of Anesthesiologists</a> (ASA) has established minimum monitoring guidelines for patients receiving general anesthesia, regional anesthesia, or sedation. These include electrocardiography (ECG), heart rate, blood pressure, inspired and expired gases, oxygen saturation of the blood (pulse oximetry), and temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASAHQ_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASAHQ-7">[7]</a></sup> In the UK the Association of Anaesthetists (AAGBI) have set minimum monitoring guidelines for general and regional anesthesia. For minor surgery, this generally includes monitoring of <a href="/wiki/Heart_rate" title="Heart rate">heart rate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_saturation" title="Oxygen saturation">oxygen saturation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a>, and inspired and expired concentrations for <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, and inhalational anesthetic agents. For more invasive surgery, monitoring may also include temperature, urine output, blood pressure, <a href="/wiki/Central_venous_pressure" title="Central venous pressure">central venous pressure</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery_pressure" title="Pulmonary artery pressure">pulmonary artery pressure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_wedge_pressure" title="Pulmonary wedge pressure">pulmonary artery occlusion pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_output" title="Cardiac output">cardiac output</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bispectral_index" title="Bispectral index">cerebral activity</a>, and neuromuscular function. In addition, the operating room environment must be monitored for ambient temperature and humidity, as well as for accumulation of exhaled inhalational anesthetic agents, which might be deleterious to the health of operating room personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAGBI_Monitoring_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAGBI_Monitoring-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sedation">Sedation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sedation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sedation" title="Sedation">Sedation</a></div>
<p>Sedation (also referred to as <i>dissociative anesthesia</i> or <i>twilight anesthesia</i>) creates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypnotic_state" title="Hypnotic state">hypnotic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sedation" title="Sedation">sedative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anxiolytic" title="Anxiolytic">anxiolytic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amnesic" title="Amnesic">amnesic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anticonvulsant" title="Anticonvulsant">anticonvulsant</a>, and centrally produced muscle-relaxing properties.  From the perspective of the person giving the sedation, the patient appears sleepy, relaxed and forgetful, allowing unpleasant procedures to be more easily completed.  Sedatives such as <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepines</a> are usually given with pain relievers (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narcotics" title="Narcotics">narcotics</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_anesthetics" title="Local anesthetics">local anesthetics</a> or both) because they do not, by themselves, provide significant <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">pain relief</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reddy_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reddy-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the perspective of the animal receiving a sedative, the effect is a feeling of general relaxation, amnesia (loss of memory) and time passing quickly.  Many drugs can produce a sedative effect including <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propofol" title="Propofol">propofol</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thiopental" title="Thiopental">thiopental</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ketamine" title="Ketamine">ketamine</a> and inhaled general anesthetics.  The advantage of sedation over a general anesthetic is that it generally does not require support of the airway or breathing (no <a href="/wiki/Tracheal_intubation" title="Tracheal intubation">tracheal intubation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_ventilation" title="Mechanical ventilation">mechanical ventilation</a>) and can have less of an effect on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_system" title="Cardiovascular system">cardiovascular system</a> which may add to a greater margin of safety in some patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 736">: 736 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_anesthesia">Regional anesthesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Regional anesthesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conduction_anesthesia" title="Conduction anesthesia">Conduction anesthesia</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:184px;max-width:184px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fermoral_nerve_block.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2808" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Fermoral_nerve_block.jpg/180px-Fermoral_nerve_block.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Fermoral_nerve_block.jpg/270px-Fermoral_nerve_block.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Fermoral_nerve_block.jpg/360px-Fermoral_nerve_block.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Sonography guided femoral nerve block</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liquor_bei_Spinalanaesthesie.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Liquor_bei_Spinalanaesthesie.JPG/180px-Liquor_bei_Spinalanaesthesie.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Liquor_bei_Spinalanaesthesie.JPG/270px-Liquor_bei_Spinalanaesthesie.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Liquor_bei_Spinalanaesthesie.JPG/360px-Liquor_bei_Spinalanaesthesie.JPG 2x" width="180"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Backflow of <a href="/wiki/Cerebrospinal_fluid" title="Cerebrospinal fluid">cerebrospinal fluid</a> through a spinal needle after puncture of the <a href="/wiki/Arachnoid_mater" title="Arachnoid mater">arachnoid mater</a> during spinal anesthesia</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>When pain is blocked from a part of the body using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_anesthetics" title="Local anesthetics">local anesthetics</a>, it is generally referred to as regional anesthesia. There are many types of regional anesthesia either by injecting into the tissue itself, a vein that feeds the area or around a nerve trunk that supplies sensation to the area. The latter are called nerve blocks and are divided into peripheral or central nerve blocks.
</p><p>The following are the types of regional anesthesia:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-11"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 926–31">: 926–31 </span></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Infiltrative anesthesia</i>: a small amount of local anesthetic is injected in a small area to stop any sensation (such as during the closure of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laceration" title="Laceration">laceration</a>, as a <a href="/wiki/Continuous_wound_infiltration" title="Continuous wound infiltration">continuous infusion</a> or "freezing" a tooth).  The effect is almost immediate.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Nerve_block" title="Nerve block">Peripheral nerve block</a></i>: local anesthetic is injected near a nerve that provides sensation to particular portion of the body.  There is significant variation in the speed of onset and duration of anesthesia depending on the potency of the drug (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Inferior_alveolar_nerve_anaesthesia" title="Inferior alveolar nerve anaesthesia">Mandibular block</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fascia_Iliaca_Compartment_Block&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fascia Iliaca Compartment Block (page does not exist)">Fascia Iliaca Compartment Block</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallinson2019_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mallinson2019-10">[10]</a></sup>).</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Intravenous_regional_anesthesia" title="Intravenous regional anesthesia">Intravenous regional anesthesia</a></i> (also called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bier_block" title="Bier block">Bier block</a>): dilute local anesthetic is infused to a limb through a vein with a <a href="/wiki/Tourniquet" title="Tourniquet">tourniquet</a> placed to prevent the drug from diffusing out of the limb.</li>
<li><i>Central nerve block</i>: Local anesthetic is injected or infused in or around a portion of the central nervous system (discussed in more detail below in spinal, epidural and caudal anesthesia).</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Topical_anesthetic" title="Topical anesthetic">Topical anesthesia</a></i>: local anesthetics that are specially formulated to diffuse through the mucous membranes or skin to give a thin layer of analgesia to an area (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Lidocaine/prilocaine" title="Lidocaine/prilocaine">EMLA patches</a>).</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Tumescent_anesthesia" title="Tumescent anesthesia">Tumescent anesthesia</a></i>: a large amount of very dilute local anesthetics are injected into the <a href="/wiki/Subcutaneous_tissue" title="Subcutaneous tissue">subcutaneous tissues</a> during liposuction.</li>
<li><i>Systemic local anesthetics</i>: local anesthetics are given systemically (orally or intravenous) to relieve <a href="/wiki/Neuropathic_pain" title="Neuropathic pain">neuropathic pain</a>.</li></ul>
<p>A 2018 Cochrane review found moderate quality evidence that regional anesthesia may reduce the frequency of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persistent_postoperative_pain" title="Persistent postoperative pain">persistent postoperative pain</a> (PPP) from three to 18 months following <a href="/wiki/Thoracotomy" title="Thoracotomy">thoracotomy</a> and three to 1 months following <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">caesarean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">[11]</a></sup> Low quality evidence was found three to 12 months following breast cancer surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">[11]</a></sup>  This review acknowledges certain limitations that impact its applicability beyond the surgeries and regional anesthesia techniques reviewed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nerve_blocks">Nerve blocks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Nerve blocks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nerve_block" title="Nerve block">Nerve block</a></div>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Local_anesthetic" title="Local anesthetic">local anesthetic</a> is injected around a larger diameter nerve that transmits sensation from an entire region it is referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Nerve_block" title="Nerve block">nerve block</a> or regional nerve blockade.  Nerve blocks are commonly used in dentistry, when the <a href="/wiki/Mandibular_nerve" title="Mandibular nerve">mandibular nerve</a> is blocked for procedures on the lower teeth.  With larger diameter nerves (such as the <a href="/wiki/Scalene_muscles" title="Scalene muscles">interscalene</a> block for upper limbs or <a href="/wiki/Psoas_major_muscle" title="Psoas major muscle">psoas compartment</a> block for lower limbs) the nerve and position of the needle is localized with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_ultrasonography" title="Medical ultrasonography">ultrasound</a> or electrical stimulation. Evidence supports the use of ultrasound guidance alone, or in combination with peripheral nerve stimulation, as superior for improved sensory and motor block, a reduction in the need for supplementation and fewer complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>  Because of the large amount of <a href="/wiki/Local_anesthetic" title="Local anesthetic">local anesthetic</a> required to affect the nerve, the maximum dose of local anesthetic has to be considered.  Nerve blocks are also used as a continuous infusion, following major surgery such as knee, hip and shoulder replacement surgery, and may be associated with lower complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullah_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullah-13">[13]</a></sup> Nerve blocks are also associated with a lower risk of neurologic complications compared to the more central epidural or spinal neuraxial blocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-12"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1639–41">: 1639–41 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Spinal.2C_epidural_and_caudal_anesthesia"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Spinal,_epidural_and_caudal_anesthesia">Spinal, epidural and caudal anesthesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Spinal, epidural and caudal anesthesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Neuraxial_blockade" title="Neuraxial blockade">Neuraxial blockade</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_neuraxial_anesthesia" title="History of neuraxial anesthesia">History of neuraxial anesthesia</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Neuraxial_blockade" title="Neuraxial blockade">Central neuraxial anesthesia</a> is the injection of <a href="/wiki/Local_anesthetic" title="Local anesthetic">local anesthetic</a> around the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a> to provide analgesia in the <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_pelvis" title="Human pelvis">pelvis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Human_leg" title="Human leg">lower extremities</a>.  It is divided into either spinal (injection into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subarachnoid_space" title="Subarachnoid space">subarachnoid space</a>), epidural (injection outside of the subarachnoid space into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidural" title="Epidural">epidural</a> space) and caudal (injection into the <a href="/wiki/Cauda_equina" title="Cauda equina">cauda equina</a> or tail end of the spinal cord).  Spinal and epidural are the most commonly used forms of central neuraxial blockade.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_anesthesia" title="Spinal anesthesia">Spinal anesthesia</a> is a "one-shot" injection that provides rapid onset and profound sensory anesthesia with lower doses of anesthetic, and is usually associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuromuscular_blockade" title="Neuromuscular blockade">neuromuscular blockade</a> (loss of muscle control). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidural_anesthesia" title="Epidural anesthesia">Epidural anesthesia</a> uses larger doses of anesthetic infused through an indwelling catheter which allows the anesthetic to be augmented should the effects begin to dissipate. Epidural anesthesia does not typically affect muscle control.
</p><p>Because central neuraxial blockade causes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial" title="Arterial">arterial</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venous" title="Venous">venous</a> <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">vasodilation</a>, a drop in <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> is common. This drop is largely dictated by the venous side of the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a> which holds 75% of the circulating <a href="/wiki/Blood_volume" title="Blood volume">blood volume</a>.  The physiologic effects are much greater when the block is placed above the 5th <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_vertebrae" title="Thoracic vertebrae">thoracic vertebra</a>.  An ineffective block is most often due to inadequate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anxiolysis" title="Anxiolysis">anxiolysis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sedation" title="Sedation">sedation</a> rather than a failure of the block itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-13"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1611">: 1611 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acute_pain_management">Acute pain management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Acute pain management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PCA-01.JPG" title="A patient-controlled analgesia infusion pump, configured for epidural administration of fentanyl and bupivacainefor postoperative analgesia"><img alt="A patient-controlled analgesia infusion pump, configured for epidural administration of fentanyl and bupivacainefor postoperative analgesia" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/PCA-01.JPG/180px-PCA-01.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/PCA-01.JPG/270px-PCA-01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/PCA-01.JPG/360px-PCA-01.JPG 2x" width="180"/></a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nociception" title="Nociception">Nociception</a> (pain sensation) is not hard-wired into the body.  Instead, it is a dynamic process wherein persistent painful stimuli can sensitize the system and either make pain management difficult or promote the development of chronic pain. For this reason, preemptive acute pain management may reduce both acute and chronic pain and is tailored to the surgery, the environment in which it is given (in-patient/out-patient) and the individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-14"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2757">: 2757 </span></sup>
</p><p>Pain management is classified into either pre-emptive or on-demand.  On-demand pain medications typically include either <a href="/wiki/Opioid" title="Opioid">opioid</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-steroidal_anti-inflammatory_drugs" title="Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs">non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs</a> but can also make use of novel approaches such as inhaled <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klomp_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klomp-14">[14]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Ketamine" title="Ketamine">ketamine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> On demand drugs can be administered by a clinician ("as needed drug orders") or by the patient using <a href="/wiki/Patient-controlled_analgesia" title="Patient-controlled analgesia">patient-controlled analgesia</a> (PCA).  PCA has been shown to provide slightly better pain control and increased patient satisfaction when compared with conventional methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hudcova_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hudcova-16">[16]</a></sup> Common preemptive approaches include epidural neuraxial blockade<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-17">[17]</a></sup> or nerve blocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klomp_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Klomp-14">[14]</a></sup> One review which looked at pain control after <a href="/wiki/Aortic_aneurysm" title="Aortic aneurysm">abdominal aortic surgery</a> found that epidural blockade provides better pain relief (especially during movement) in the period up to three postoperative days. It reduces the duration of postoperative <a href="/wiki/Tracheal_intubation" title="Tracheal intubation">tracheal intubation</a> by roughly half. The occurrence of prolonged postoperative <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_ventilation" title="Mechanical ventilation">mechanical ventilation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">myocardial infarction</a> is also reduced by epidural analgesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26731032_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26731032-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Risks_and_complications">Risks and complications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Risks and complications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Patient_safety" title="Patient safety">Patient safety</a></div>
<p>Risks and complications as they relate to anesthesia are classified as either morbidity (a disease or disorder that results from anesthesia) or <a href="/wiki/Perioperative_mortality" title="Perioperative mortality">mortality</a> (death that results from anesthesia).  Quantifying how anesthesia contributes to morbidity and mortality can be difficult because a animal's health prior to surgery and the complexity of the surgical procedure can also contribute to the risks.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mortality_rates_by_ASA_status_from_Anesthesiology,_V_97,_No_6,_Dec_2002_p1615.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1654" data-file-width="2521" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Mortality_rates_by_ASA_status_from_Anesthesiology%2C_V_97%2C_No_6%2C_Dec_2002_p1615.png/220px-Mortality_rates_by_ASA_status_from_Anesthesiology%2C_V_97%2C_No_6%2C_Dec_2002_p1615.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Mortality_rates_by_ASA_status_from_Anesthesiology%2C_V_97%2C_No_6%2C_Dec_2002_p1615.png/330px-Mortality_rates_by_ASA_status_from_Anesthesiology%2C_V_97%2C_No_6%2C_Dec_2002_p1615.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Mortality_rates_by_ASA_status_from_Anesthesiology%2C_V_97%2C_No_6%2C_Dec_2002_p1615.png/440px-Mortality_rates_by_ASA_status_from_Anesthesiology%2C_V_97%2C_No_6%2C_Dec_2002_p1615.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mortality_rates_by_ASA_status_from_Anesthesiology,_V_97,_No_6,_Dec_2002_p1615.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anesthesia-related deaths by <a href="/wiki/ASA_physical_status_classification_system" title="ASA physical status classification system">ASA status</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lagasse_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lagasse-19">[19]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Prior to the introduction of anesthesia in the early 19th century, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(physiology)" title="Stress (physiology)">physiologic stress</a> from surgery caused significant complications and many deaths from <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">shock</a>.  The faster the surgery was, the lower the rate of complications (leading to reports of very quick amputations).  The advent of anesthesia allowed more complicated and life-saving surgery to be completed, decreased the physiologic stress of the surgery, but added an element of risk.  It was two years after the introduction of ether anesthetics that the first death directly related to the use of anesthesia was reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaloner_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaloner-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Morbidity can be major (<a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">myocardial infarction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_embolism" title="Pulmonary embolism">pulmonary embolism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kidney_failure" title="Kidney failure">kidney failure</a>/<a href="/wiki/Chronic_kidney_disease" title="Chronic kidney disease">chronic kidney disease</a>, postoperative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive_dysfunction" title="Cognitive dysfunction">cognitive dysfunction</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allergic_reactions_to_anaesthesia" title="Allergic reactions to anaesthesia">allergy</a>) or minor (minor <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>, vomiting, readmission).  There is usually overlap in the contributing factors that lead to morbidity and mortality between the health of the animals, the type of surgery being performed and the anesthetic.  To understand the <a href="/wiki/Relative_risk" title="Relative risk">relative risk</a> of each contributing factor, consider that the rate of deaths totally attributed to the patient's health is 1:870.  Compare that to the rate of deaths totally attributed to surgical factors (1:2860) or anesthesia alone (1:185,056) illustrating that the single greatest factor in anesthetic mortality is the health of the patient.  These statistics can also be compared to the first such study on mortality in anesthesia from 1954, which reported a rate of death from all causes at 1:75 and a rate attributed to anesthesia alone at 1:2680.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-15"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 993">: 993 </span></sup>  Direct comparisons between mortality statistics cannot reliably be made over time and across countries because of differences in the stratification of risk factors, however, there is evidence that anesthetics have made a significant improvement in safety<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braz_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Braz-21">[21]</a></sup> but to what degree is uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lagasse_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lagasse-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rather than stating a flat rate of morbidity or mortality, many factors are reported as contributing to the <a href="/wiki/Relative_risk" title="Relative risk">relative risk</a> of the procedure and anesthetic combined.  For instance, an operation on a person who is between the ages of 60–79 years old places the patient at 2.3 times greater risk than someone less than 60 years old.  Having an ASA score of 3, 4 or 5 places the person at 10.7 times greater risk than someone with an ASA score of 1 or 2.  Other variables include age greater than 80 (3.3 times risk compared to those under 60), gender (females have a lower risk of 0.8), urgency of the procedure (emergencies have a 4.4 times greater risk), experience of the person completing the procedure (less than 8 years experience and/or less than 600 cases have a 1.1 times greater risk) and the type of anesthetic (regional anesthetics are lower risk than general anesthetics).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-16"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 984">: 984 </span></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obstetrical" title="Obstetrical">Obstetrical</a>, the very young and the very old are all at greater risk of complication so extra precautions may need to be taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-17"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 969–86">: 969–86 </span></sup>
</p><p>On 14 December 2016, the Food and Drug Administration issued a Public Safety Communication warning that "repeated or lengthy use of general anesthetic and sedation drugs during surgeries or procedures in children younger than 3 years or in pregnant women during their third trimester may affect the development of children's brains."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The warning was criticized by the American College of Obstetricians and Gynecologists, which pointed out the absence of direct evidence regarding use in pregnant women and the possibility that "this warning could inappropriately dissuade providers from providing medically indicated care during pregnancy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Patient advocates noted that a randomized clinical trial would be unethical, that the mechanism of injury is well-established in animals, and that studies had shown exposure to multiple uses of anesthetic significantly increased the risk of developing learning disabilities in young children, with a <a href="/wiki/Hazard_ratio" title="Hazard ratio">hazard ratio</a> of 2.12 (95% confidence interval, 1.26–3.54).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recovery">Recovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Recovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The immediate time after anesthesia is called <a href="/wiki/General_anaesthesia#Emergence" title="General anaesthesia">emergence</a>.  Emergence from general anesthesia or sedation requires careful monitoring because there is still a risk of complication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAGBI_Recovery_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAGBI_Recovery-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">Nausea</a> and vomiting are reported at 9.8% but will vary with the type of anesthetic and procedure.  There is a need for <a href="/wiki/Airway_management" title="Airway management">airway support</a> in 6.8%, there can be <a href="/wiki/Urinary_retention" title="Urinary retention">urinary retention</a> (more common in those over 50 years of age) and <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">hypotension</a> in 2.7%.  <a href="/wiki/Hypothermia" title="Hypothermia">Hypothermia</a>, shivering and confusion are also common in the immediate post-operative period because of the lack of muscle movement (and subsequent lack of heat production) during the procedure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-18"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2707">: 2707 </span></sup> Furthermore, the rare manifestation in the post-anesthetic period may be the occurrence of functional neurological symptom disorder (FNSD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Postoperative_cognitive_dysfunction" title="Postoperative cognitive dysfunction">Postoperative cognitive dysfunction</a> (also known as <i>POCD</i> and post-anesthetic confusion) is a disturbance in <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a> after surgery.  It may also be variably used to describe <a href="/wiki/Emergence_delirium" title="Emergence delirium">emergence delirium</a> (immediate post-operative confusion) and early cognitive dysfunction (diminished cognitive function in the first post-operative week).  Although the three entities (delirium, early POCD and long-term POCD) are separate, the presence of delirium post-operatively predicts the presence of early POCD.  There does not appear to be an association between delirium or early POCD and long-term POCD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudolph2008_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rudolph2008-27">[27]</a></sup> According to a recent study conducted at the David Geffen School of Medicine at UCLA, the brain navigates its way through a series of activity clusters, or "hubs" on its way back to consciousness. Dr. Andrew Hudson, an assistant professor in anesthesiology states, "Recovery from anesthesia is not simply the result of the anesthetic 'wearing off,' but also of the brain finding its way back through a maze of possible activity states to those that allow conscious experience. Put simply, the brain reboots itself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Long-term POCD is a subtle deterioration in cognitive function, that can last for weeks, months, or longer. Most commonly, relatives of the person report a lack of attention, memory and loss of interest in activities previously dear to the person (such as crosswords). In a similar way, people in the workforce may report an inability to complete tasks at the same speed they could previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deiner_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deiner-29">[29]</a></sup> There is good evidence that POCD occurs after cardiac surgery and the major reason for its occurrence is the formation of <a href="/wiki/Embolism" title="Embolism">microemboli</a>. POCD also appears to occur in non-cardiac surgery.  Its causes in non-cardiac surgery are less clear but older age is a risk factor for its occurrence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2010_2-19"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2805–16">: 2805–16 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_general_anesthesia" title="History of general anesthesia">History of general anesthesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_neuraxial_anesthesia" title="History of neuraxial anesthesia">History of neuraxial anesthesia</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hua_Tuo" title="Hua Tuo">Hua Tuo</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Hua_Tuo_in_GDMU.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Statue_of_Hua_Tuo_in_GDMU.jpg/220px-Statue_of_Hua_Tuo_in_GDMU.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Statue_of_Hua_Tuo_in_GDMU.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Hua_Tuo_in_GDMU.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Statue_of_Hua_Tuo_in_GDMU.jpg/440px-Statue_of_Hua_Tuo_in_GDMU.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Hua_Tuo_in_GDMU.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hua Tuo</div></div></div>
<p>The first attempts at general anesthesia were probably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbalism" title="Herbalism">herbal remedies</a> administered in <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">Alcohol</a> is one of the oldest known <a href="/wiki/Sedative" title="Sedative">sedatives</a> and it was used in ancient <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> thousands of years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powell1996_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Powell1996-30">[30]</a></sup> The Sumerians are said to have cultivated and harvested the <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a> poppy (<i><a href="/wiki/Papaver_somniferum" title="Papaver somniferum">Papaver somniferum</a></i>) in lower Mesopotamia as early as 3400 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">BCE</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neligan1927_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neligan1927-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booth1996_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Booth1996-32">[32]</a></sup> The ancient Egyptians had some surgical instruments,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebers1889_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ebers1889-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pahor1992I_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pahor1992I-34">[34]</a></sup> as well as crude analgesics and sedatives, including possibly an extract prepared from the <a href="/wiki/Mandragora_(genus)" title="Mandragora (genus)">mandrake</a> fruit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan1996_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan1996-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>In China, <a href="/wiki/Bian_Que" title="Bian Que">Bian Que</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese</a>: 扁鹊, <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i>Pien Ch'iao</i>, c. 300 BCE) was a legendary Chinese <a href="/wiki/Internal_medicine" title="Internal medicine">internist</a> and surgeon who reportedly used general anesthesia for surgical procedures.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Despite this, it was the Chinese physician <a href="/wiki/Hua_Tuo" title="Hua Tuo">Hua Tuo</a> whom historians considered the first verifiable historical figure to develop a type of mixture of anesthesia, though his recipe has yet to be fully discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout Europe, Asia, and the Americas, a variety of <i><a href="/wiki/Solanum" title="Solanum">Solanum</a></i> species containing potent <a href="/wiki/Tropane_alkaloid" title="Tropane alkaloid">tropane alkaloids</a> was used for anesthesia. In 13th-century Italy, <a href="/wiki/Theodoric_Borgognoni" title="Theodoric Borgognoni">Theodoric Borgognoni</a> used similar mixtures along with opiates to induce unconsciousness, and treatment with the combined alkaloids proved a mainstay of anesthesia until the 19th century.  Local anesthetics were used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca_civilization" title="Inca civilization">Inca civilization</a> where <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamans</a> chewed <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">coca</a> leaves and performed operations on the skull while spitting into the wounds they had inflicted to anesthetize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruetsch2001_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruetsch2001-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">Cocaine</a> was later isolated and became the first effective local anesthetic.  It was first used in 1859 by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Koller_(ophthalmologist)" title="Karl Koller (ophthalmologist)">Karl Koller</a>, at the suggestion of <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Eye_surgery" title="Eye surgery">eye surgery</a> in 1884.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koller1884_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koller1884-38">[38]</a></sup> German surgeon <a href="/wiki/August_Bier" title="August Bier">August Bier</a> (1861–1949) was the first to use cocaine for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intrathecal" title="Intrathecal">intrathecal</a> anesthesia in 1898.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bier1899_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bier1899-39">[39]</a></sup> Romanian surgeon Nicolae Racoviceanu-Piteşti (1860–1942) was the first to use <a href="/wiki/Opioid" title="Opioid">opioids</a> for intrathecal analgesia; he presented his experience in Paris in 1901.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brill2003_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brill2003-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early medieval Arabic writings mention anaesthesia by inhalation. <a href="/wiki/Inhalational_anesthetic" title="Inhalational anesthetic">Inhalational anesthetics</a> were first used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medicine_in_medieval_Islam" title="Medicine in medieval Islam">Arabic physicians</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abulcasis" title="Abulcasis">Abulcasis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Zuhr" title="Ibn Zuhr">Ibn Zuhr</a> and Persian physician <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> in the 11th century. They used a sponge soaked with narcotic drugs and placed it on a patient's face.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> These Arabic physicians were the first to use an anaesthetic sponge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunke1969_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hunke1969-42">[42]</a></sup> Arab/Persian physicians also introduced the use of preoperative <a href="/wiki/General_anaesthetic" title="General anaesthetic">anaesthetic compounds</a> around the 9th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabriel210_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gabriel210-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The "soporific sponge" ("sleep sponge") used by Arabic physicians was introduced to Europe by the <a href="/wiki/Schola_Medica_Salernitana" title="Schola Medica Salernitana">Salerno school of medicine</a> in the late 12th century and by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ugo_Borgognoni" title="Ugo Borgognoni">Ugo Borgognoni</a> (1180–1258) in the 13th century. The sponge was promoted and described by Ugo's son and fellow surgeon, <a href="/wiki/Theodoric_Borgognoni" title="Theodoric Borgognoni">Theodoric Borgognoni</a> (1205–1298). In this anesthetic method, a sponge was soaked in a dissolved solution of opium, <a href="/wiki/Mandrake" title="Mandrake">mandragora</a>, hemlock juice, and other substances. The sponge was then dried and stored; just before surgery the sponge was moistened and then held under the patient's nose. When all went well, the fumes rendered the individual unconscious.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
