<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anaesthesia_exhibition,_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2888" data-file-width="3995" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg/220px-Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg/330px-Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg/440px-Anaesthesia_exhibition%2C_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anaesthesia_exhibition,_1946_Wellcome_M0009908.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a>'s <i>Researches chemical and philosophical: chiefly concerning nitrous oxide</i> (1800), pp. 556 and 557 (right), outlining potential anesthetic properties of <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> in relieving pain during surgery.</div></div></div>
<p>The most famous anesthetic, <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether#history" title="Diethyl ether">ether</a>, may have been synthesized as early as the 8th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barash_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barash-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lullus_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lullus-45">[45]</a></sup> but it took many centuries for its anesthetic importance to be appreciated, even though the 16th century physician and polymath <a href="/wiki/Paracelsus" title="Paracelsus">Paracelsus</a> noted that chickens made to breathe it not only fell asleep but also felt no pain. By the early 19th century, ether was being used by humans, but only as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recreational_drug" title="Recreational drug">recreational drug</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenster2001_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fenster2001-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, in 1772, English scientist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> discovered the gas <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>. Initially, people thought this gas to be lethal, even in small doses, like some other <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxides</a>. However, in 1799, British chemist and inventor <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> decided to find out by experimenting on himself. To his astonishment he found that nitrous oxide made him laugh, so he nicknamed it "laughing gas".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davy_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davy-47">[47]</a></sup> In 1800 Davy wrote about the potential anesthetic properties of nitrous oxide in relieving pain during surgery, but nobody at that time pursued the matter any further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davy_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davy-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 14 November 1804, <a href="/wiki/Hanaoka_Seish%C5%AB" title="Hanaoka Seishū">Hanaoka Seishū</a>, a Japanese doctor, became the first person to successfully perform surgery using <a href="/wiki/General_anaesthesia" title="General anaesthesia">general anesthesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Hanaoka learned traditional Japanese medicine as well as <a href="/wiki/Rangaku" title="Rangaku">Dutch-imported</a> European surgery and Chinese medicine.  After years of research and experimentation, he finally developed a formula which he named tsūsensan (also known as mafutsu-san), which combined <a href="/wiki/Datura_stramonium" title="Datura stramonium">Korean morning glory</a> and other herbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hanaoka's success in performing this painless operation soon became widely known, and patients began to arrive from all parts of Japan. Hanaoka went on to perform many operations using tsūsensan, including resection of <a href="/wiki/Malignancy" title="Malignancy">malignant</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">tumors</a>, extraction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urolithiasis" title="Urolithiasis">bladder stones</a>, and extremity amputations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Before his death in 1835, Hanaoka performed more than 150 operations for breast cancer. However, this finding did not benefit the rest of the world until 1854 as the <a href="/wiki/Sakoku" title="Sakoku">national isolation policy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a> prevented Hanaoka's achievements from being publicized until after the isolation ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Nearly forty years would pass before <a href="/wiki/Crawford_Long" title="Crawford Long">Crawford Long</a>, who is titled as the inventor of modern anesthetics in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">West</a>, used general anesthesia in <a href="/wiki/Jefferson,_Georgia" title="Jefferson, Georgia">Jefferson, Georgia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Long noticed that his friends felt no pain when they injured themselves while staggering around under the influence of diethyl ether. He immediately thought of its potential in surgery. Conveniently, a participant in one of those "ether frolics", a student named James Venable, had two small tumors he wanted excised. But fearing the pain of surgery, Venable kept putting the operation off. Hence, Long suggested that he have his operation while under the influence of ether. Venable agreed, and on 30 March 1842 he underwent a painless operation. However, Long did not announce his discovery until 1849.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long1849_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Long1849-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Southworth_%26_Hawes_-_First_etherized_operation_(re-enactment).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4826" data-file-width="6006" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Southworth_%26_Hawes_-_First_etherized_operation_%28re-enactment%29.jpg/220px-Southworth_%26_Hawes_-_First_etherized_operation_%28re-enactment%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Southworth_%26_Hawes_-_First_etherized_operation_%28re-enactment%29.jpg/330px-Southworth_%26_Hawes_-_First_etherized_operation_%28re-enactment%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Southworth_%26_Hawes_-_First_etherized_operation_%28re-enactment%29.jpg/440px-Southworth_%26_Hawes_-_First_etherized_operation_%28re-enactment%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Southworth_%26_Hawes_-_First_etherized_operation_(re-enactment).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporary re-enactment of Morton's 16 October 1846, <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">ether</a> operation; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daguerrotype" title="Daguerrotype">daguerrotype</a> by <a href="/wiki/Southworth_%26_Hawes" title="Southworth &amp; Hawes">Southworth &amp; Hawes</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ether_inhaler,_c._1846,_developed_by_William_T._G._Morton_-_National_Museum_of_American_History_-_DSC06167.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3837" data-file-width="2817" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ether_inhaler%2C_c._1846%2C_developed_by_William_T._G._Morton_-_National_Museum_of_American_History_-_DSC06167.JPG/220px-Ether_inhaler%2C_c._1846%2C_developed_by_William_T._G._Morton_-_National_Museum_of_American_History_-_DSC06167.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ether_inhaler%2C_c._1846%2C_developed_by_William_T._G._Morton_-_National_Museum_of_American_History_-_DSC06167.JPG/330px-Ether_inhaler%2C_c._1846%2C_developed_by_William_T._G._Morton_-_National_Museum_of_American_History_-_DSC06167.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ether_inhaler%2C_c._1846%2C_developed_by_William_T._G._Morton_-_National_Museum_of_American_History_-_DSC06167.JPG/440px-Ether_inhaler%2C_c._1846%2C_developed_by_William_T._G._Morton_-_National_Museum_of_American_History_-_DSC06167.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ether_inhaler,_c._1846,_developed_by_William_T._G._Morton_-_National_Museum_of_American_History_-_DSC06167.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Morton's ether inhaler</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Horace_Wells" title="Horace Wells">Horace Wells</a> conducted the first public demonstration of the inhalational anesthetic at the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_General_Hospital" title="Massachusetts General Hospital">Massachusetts General Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> in 1845. However, the <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> was improperly administered and the person cried out in <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMAH_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-NMAH-54">[54]</a></sup> On 16 October 1846, Boston dentist <a href="/wiki/William_T._G._Morton" title="William T. G. Morton">William Thomas Green Morton</a> gave a successful demonstration using <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">diethyl ether</a> to medical students at the same venue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Morton, who was unaware of Long's previous work, was invited to the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_General_Hospital" title="Massachusetts General Hospital">Massachusetts General Hospital</a> to demonstrate his new technique for painless surgery. After Morton had induced anesthesia, surgeon <a href="/wiki/John_Collins_Warren" title="John Collins Warren">John Collins Warren</a> removed a tumor from the neck of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gilbert_Abbott" title="Edward Gilbert Abbott">Edward Gilbert Abbott</a>. This occurred in the surgical amphitheater now called the <a href="/wiki/Ether_Dome" title="Ether Dome">Ether Dome</a>. The previously skeptical Warren was impressed and stated, "Gentlemen, this is no humbug." In a letter to Morton shortly thereafter, physician and writer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oliver_Wendell_Holmes,_Sr." title="Oliver Wendell Holmes, Sr.">Oliver Wendell Holmes, Sr.</a> proposed naming the state produced "anesthesia", and the procedure an "anesthetic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fenster2001_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fenster2001-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Morton at first attempted to hide the actual nature of his anesthetic substance, referring to it as Letheon. He received a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_patent" title="US patent">US patent</a> for his substance, but news of the successful anesthetic spread quickly by late 1846. Respected surgeons in Europe including <a href="/wiki/Robert_Liston" title="Robert Liston">Liston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Dieffenbach" title="Johann Friedrich Dieffenbach">Dieffenbach</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikolay_Ivanovich_Pirogov" title="Nikolay Ivanovich Pirogov">Pirogov</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_Syme" title="James Syme">Syme</a> quickly undertook numerous operations with ether. An American-born physician, Boott, encouraged London dentist <a href="/wiki/James_Robinson_(dentist)" title="James Robinson (dentist)">James Robinson</a> to perform a dental procedure on a Miss Lonsdale. This was the first case of an operator-anesthetist. On the same day, 19 December 1846, in Dumfries Royal Infirmary, Scotland, a Dr. Scott used ether for a surgical procedure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The first use of anesthesia in the Southern Hemisphere took place in <a href="/wiki/Launceston,_Tasmania" title="Launceston, Tasmania">Launceston, Tasmania</a>, that same year. Drawbacks with ether such as excessive vomiting and its explosive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flammability" title="Flammability">flammability</a> led to its replacement in England with <a href="/wiki/Chloroform" title="Chloroform">chloroform</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Discovered in 1831 by an American physician Samuel Guthrie (1782–1848), and independently a few months later by Frenchman Eugène Soubeiran (1797–1859) and Justus von Liebig (1803–1873) in Germany, chloroform was named and chemically characterized in 1834 by Jean-Baptiste Dumas (1800–1884). In 1842, Dr <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mortimer_Glover" title="Robert Mortimer Glover">Robert Mortimer Glover</a> in London discovered the anaesthetic qualities of chloroform on laboratory animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1847, Scottish obstetrician <a href="/wiki/James_Young_Simpson" title="James Young Simpson">James Young Simpson</a> was the first to demonstrate the anesthetic properties of chloroform on humans and helped to popularize the drug for use in medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-eb-58">[58]</a></sup> This first supply came from local pharmacists, James Duncan and <a href="/wiki/William_Flockhart" title="William Flockhart">William Flockhart</a>, and its use spread quickly, with 750,000 doses weekly in Britain by 1895. Simpson arranged for Flockhart to supply <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale" title="Florence Nightingale">Florence Nightingale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Chloroform gained royal approval in 1853 when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Snow_(physician)" title="John Snow (physician)">John Snow</a> administered it to <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> when she was in labor with  <a href="/wiki/Prince_Leopold,_Duke_of_Albany" title="Prince Leopold, Duke of Albany">Prince Leopold</a>. For the experience of child birth itself, chloroform met all the Queen's expectations; she stated it was "delightful beyond measure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>  Chloroform was not without fault though. The first fatality directly attributed to chloroform administration was recorded on 28 January 1848 after the death of Hannah Greener.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> This was the first of many deaths to follow from the untrained handling of chloroform. Surgeons began to appreciate the need for a trained anesthetist. The need, as Thatcher writes, was for an anesthetist to "(1)Be satisfied with the subordinate role that the work would require, (2) Make anesthesia their one absorbing interest, (3) not look at the situation of anesthetist as one that put them in a position to watch and learn from the surgeons technique (4) accept the comparatively low pay and (5) have the natural aptitude and intelligence to develop a high level of skill in providing the smooth anesthesia and relaxation that the surgeon demanded"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> These qualities of an anesthetist were often found in submissive <a href="/wiki/Medical_school" title="Medical school">medical students</a> and even members of the public.  More often, surgeons sought out nurses to provide anesthesia. By the time of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>, many nurses had been professionally trained with the support of surgeons.
</p><p>John Snow of London published articles from May 1848 onwards "On Narcotism by the Inhalation of Vapours" in the London Medical Gazette.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Snow also involved himself in the production of equipment needed for the administration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inhalational_anesthetics" title="Inhalational anesthetics">inhalational anesthetics</a>, the forerunner of today's <a href="/wiki/Anaesthetic_machine" title="Anaesthetic machine">anesthesia machines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alice Magaw, born in November 1860, is often referred to as "The Mother of Anesthesia". Her renown as the personal anesthesia provider for William and Charles Mayo was solidified by Mayo's own words in his 1905 article in which he described his satisfaction with and reliance on nurse anesthetists: "The question of anaesthesia is a most important one. We have regular anaesthetists [on] whom we can depend so that I can devote my entire attention to the surgical work." Magaw kept thorough records of her cases and recorded these anesthetics. In her publication reviewing more than 14,000 surgical anesthetics, Magaw indicates she successfully provided anesthesia without an anesthetic-related death. Magaw describes
in another article, "We have administered an anesthetic 1,092 times; ether alone 674 times; chloroform 245 times; ether and chloroform combined 173 times. I can report that out of this number, 1,092 cases, we have not had an accident". Magaw's records and outcomes created a legacy defining that the delivery of anesthesia by nurses would serve the surgical community without increasing the risks to patients. In fact, Magaw's outcomes would eclipse those of practitioners today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first comprehensive medical textbook on the subject, <i>Anesthesia</i>, was authored in 1914 by anesthesiologist Dr. <a href="/wiki/James_Tayloe_Gwathmey" title="James Tayloe Gwathmey">James Tayloe Gwathmey</a> and the chemist Dr. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Baskerville" title="Charles Baskerville">Charles Baskerville</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-66">[66]</a></sup> This book served as the standard reference for the specialty for decades and included details on the history of anesthesia as well as the physiology and techniques of inhalation, rectal, intravenous, and spinal anesthesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Of these first famous anesthetics, only nitrous oxide is still widely used today, with chloroform and ether having been replaced by safer but sometimes more expensive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_anesthetic" title="General anesthetic">general anesthetics</a>, and cocaine by more effective <a href="/wiki/Local_anesthetic" title="Local anesthetic">local anesthetics</a> with less abuse potential.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia_provision_in_the_United_States" title="Anesthesia provision in the United States">Anesthesia provision in the United States</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anesthesiologist" title="Anesthesiologist">Anesthesiologist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nurse_anesthetist" title="Nurse anesthetist">Nurse anesthetist</a></div>
<p>Almost all healthcare providers use anesthetic drugs to some degree, but most health professions have their own field of specialists in the field including medicine, nursing and dentistry.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">Doctors</a> specializing in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaesthesiology" title="Anaesthesiology">anaesthesiology</a>, including perioperative care, development of an anesthetic plan, and the administration of anesthetics are known in the US as <i>anesthesiologists</i> and in the UK, Canada, Australia, and NZ as <i>anaesthetists</i> or <i>anaesthesiologists</i>. All anesthetics in the UK, Australia, New Zealand, Hong Kong and Japan are administered by doctors. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nurse_anesthetists" title="Nurse anesthetists">Nurse anesthetists</a> also administer anesthesia in 109 nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IFNA_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-IFNA-67">[67]</a></sup> In the US, 35% of anesthetics are provided by physicians in solo practice, about 55% are provided by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia_care_team&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Anesthesia care team (page does not exist)">anesthesia care teams</a> (ACTs) with anesthesiologists medically directing <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Certified_registered_nurse_anesthetists&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Certified registered nurse anesthetists (page does not exist)">certified registered nurse anesthetists</a> (CRNAs) or anesthesiologist assistants, and about 10% are provided by CRNAs in solo practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IFNA_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-IFNA-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Physician2007_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Physician2007-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-When2007_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-When2007-69">[69]</a></sup> There can also be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anesthesiologist_assistant" title="Anesthesiologist assistant">anesthesiologist assistants</a> (US) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physicians%27_assistant_(anaesthesia)" title="Physicians' assistant (anaesthesia)">physicians' assistants (anaesthesia)</a> (UK) who assist with anesthesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Five_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Five-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_populations">Special populations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anesthesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Special populations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are many circumstances when anesthesia needs to be altered for special circumstances due to the procedure (such as in <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_surgery" title="Cardiac surgery">cardiac surgery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiothoracic_anesthesiology" title="Cardiothoracic anesthesiology">cardiothoracic anesthesiology</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neurosurgery" title="Neurosurgery">neurosurgery</a>), the patient (such as in <a href="/wiki/Pediatrics" title="Pediatrics">pediatric anesthesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geriatric_anesthesia" title="Geriatric anesthesia">geriatric</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bariatric" title="Bariatric">bariatric</a> or <a href="/wiki/Obstetrics" title="Obstetrics">obstetrical anesthesia</a>) or special circumstances (such as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trauma_(medicine)" title="Trauma (medicine)">trauma</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehospital_care" title="Prehospital care">prehospital care</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robotic_surgery" title="Robotic surgery">robotic surgery</a> or extreme environments).
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Hunke1969-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Hunke1969_42-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source" id="CITEREFHunke1960"><a href="/wiki/Sigrid_Hunke" title="Sigrid Hunke">Hunke S</a> (1960). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=wlIuAAAAIAAJ&amp;q=%22Allahs+Sonne+%C3%BCber+dem+Abendland:+unser+arabisches+Erbe%22" rel="nofollow"><i>Allahs Sonne über dem Abendland: unser arabisches Erbe</i></a> (in German) (2 ed.). Stuttgart: Deutsche Verlags-Anstalt. pp. 279–80. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-3-596-23543-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-3-596-23543-8"><bdi>978-3-596-23543-8</bdi></a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">13 September</span> 2010</span>. <q>The science of medicine has gained a great and extremely important discovery and that is the use of general anaesthetics for surgical operations, and how unique, efficient, and merciful for those who tried it the Muslim anaesthetic was. It was quite different from the drinks the Indians, Romans and Greeks were forcing their patients to have for relief of pain. There had been some allegations to credit this discovery to an Italian or to an Alexandrian, but the truth is and history proves that, the art of using the anaesthetic sponge is a pure Muslim technique, which was not known before. The sponge used to be dipped and left in a mixture prepared from cannabis, opium, hyoscyamus and a plant called Zoan.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Allahs+Sonne+%C3%BCber+dem+Abendland%3A+unser+arabisches+Erbe&amp;rft.place=Stuttgart&amp;rft.pages=279-80&amp;rft.edition=2&amp;rft.pub=Deutsche+Verlags-Anstalt&amp;rft.date=1960&amp;rft.isbn=978-3-596-23543-8&amp;rft.aulast=Hunke&amp;rft.aufirst=S&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DwlIuAAAAIAAJ%26q%3D%2522Allahs%2BSonne%2B%25C3%25BCber%2Bdem%2BAbendland%3A%2Bunser%2Barabisches%2BErbe%2522&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnesthesia"></span></span>
